‐ to share their limited financial resources;

‐ to reduce the entry costs to new markets;

‐ to build on the linguistic capabilities of a partner, which facilitates communication with clients from the private and public sector.

The Commission regrets that the European Economic Interest Groupings have not yet received the success they deserve and that the number so far created falls far below expectation.

In order to promote the EEIG, the Commission will publish shortly a practical handbook on the EEIGs in all working languages of the EU, containing a model contract, so that entrepreneurs may better understand the advantages of the EEIG.

In order to allow SMEs to take better advantage of the single market and facilitate the creation of transnational Joint ventures by SMEs within the Community, the European Commission created in November 1997 the Joint European Venture (JEV) Programme. This programme then became one of the three facilities of the European Growth and Employment Initiative, which was prepared on request by the European Parliament and adopted by the Council (Decision 98/347/98) on measures of financial assistance for innovative and job‐creating SMEs. This three‐year programme has earmarked EUR 80‐100 million for JEV. JEV is run by a network of 80 financial intermediaries and therefore also accessible for SMEs in the regions. This network is open‐ended and the Commission hopes that new financial intermediaries will join it.

Under JEV the Commission cofinances feasibility studies and provides a 10 % investment support up to a maximum of EUR 100 000 per project.

Therefore, in addition to the first‐stop‐shop network (Euro‐Info‐Centre) and partner search instruments such as BC‐Net, BRE, INTERPRISE workshops, EUROPARTENARIAT and IBEX events, the Community has now an operational instrument to support cross‐border investments of SMEs.

If the conditions of the above‐mentioned Council Decision are met, then SMEs who want to create a European Economic Interest Grouping which will have to be operational for at least five years, can introduce a request for support to one of the members of the financial intermediary network.

SMEs wishing to establish a transnational joint venture face particular difficulties with regard to access to finance, as banks do not have sufficient information about the quality of the foreign partners and consider a joint venture to be of higher risk. Therefore the JEV network of financial intermediaries helps SMEs to have easier access to finance as support under JEV allows to prepare better business plans.

With regard to the public procurement markets in other Member States it is well known that those markets are very difficult for SMEs and only the best SMEs which know already the private markets of those Member States have a real chance to get a contract. It should be noted that the Commission, in its Communication on Public Procurement in the European Union (COM(98)143 of 11 March 1998), has already proposed a set of measures aimed at the development of a favourable environment for businesses, and in particular SMEs.

Concerning access to credit, the introduction of the euro will increase the chances of SMEs to get more easily a loan from banks established in other Member States. However, given the diversity of bank traditions and cultures each bank applies risk assessment methods which are quite diverse, and the credit rating of SMEs by independent rating agencies is still too expensive.

Within the framework of the 3rd Round Table of Bankers and SMEs launched in October 1998, the Commission encourages the exchange of best experiences in the field of access to credits for business creators, women entrepreneurs and young entrepreneurs. In addition to loan financing, also access to equity such as business angels, seed capital and venture capital will be considered. The results will be available in November 1999.

Finally, it should be added that under the abovementioned Growth and Employment initiative, the Council has reserved EUR 320 million for a start‐up facility and an SME guarantee facility.

Question No 67 by James Nicholson (H−1219/98) Subject: SPARD Capital Grants Scheme

Is the Commission aware that the United Kingdom Government has decided against reopening the SPARD Capital Grants Scheme to applications, with the result that the Department of Agriculture for Northern Ireland is unable to receive £7.5 million of European funds, and what is the Commission's view of the implications of this decision?

 Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the decision of the United Kingdom Government not to re‐open the SPARD Capital Grants Scheme to further applications. This matter has been discussed in the relevant Working Committee and the Monitoring Committee for the Single Programming Document 1994‐1999 for Northern Ireland, where the Commission has expressed disappointment that the commitment entered into in the SPD cannot be honoured.

Question No 68 by Charlotte Cederschiöld (H−1221/98) Subject: Human rights

It is stated in the Treaty of Amsterdam that the development of an area of freedom, security and justice will be one of the EU's next objectives. There should be a body to which members of the public who feel that their human rights are being violated can take their grievances. Appeals via national legal authorities and the European Court of Justice take a long time, and the Ombudsman cannot alter that fact. A proposal has been submitted for a European authority which does not have a general supervisory role but is able to give the public advance notice if it is likely that a Member State, through certain of its actions, will violate human rights. The filing of 5000 reports against Sweden recently resulted in an inquiry into the legal situation. The legal shortcomings became clear, and Commissioner Wulf‐Mathies pointed out in plenary that the Community's powers in this area were not adequate.

Does the Commission consider that members of the public should be given greater opportunity to have their basic human rights situation reviewed and, if so, how?

 Answer

The question raised by the honourable Member concerns the implementation of the Treaty of Amsterdam, which is currently being ratified. In the meantime, the Commission is studying ways of ensuring its implementation when the time comes. It shall not fail to communicate the option chosen at the appropriate time.

Question No 69 by Jean Querbes (H−1228/98) Subject: Postponement of the abolition of intra‐Community duty‐free shopping

On 19 May 1998 the Council confirmed that the decision it took in 1991 to abolish intra‐Community duty‐free shopping would indeed be implemented on 1 July 1999. All the studies that have been carried out highlight the very large number of job losses which will result from the abolition of duty‐free shopping in airports and on ferries. In France, for example, more than 12 000 jobs would disappear in regions where unemployment levels are already very high.

Is the Commission prepared to draw the appropriate conclusions from these impact studies and to propose to the Council that the actual implementation of the decision abolishing intra‐Community duty‐free sales should be postponed?

 Answer

The Commission has received quite a number of studies relating to the impact that the abolition of duty‐free shopping will have on jobs. These studies all reach very different conclusions, according to whether they were issued by the lobby group concerned or by other, more neutral, bodies.

The European Council has just called on the Commission and the ECOFIN Council to examine, by March, the problems that the abolition of intra‐Community duty‐free shopping could pose for employment.

The Commission does indeed intend to respond to this invitation and, once it has examined the situation, it will submit its conclusions to the ECOFIN Council. As the examination has not yet been completed it would be premature to comment on the result.

Question No 70 by Antoinette Spaak (H−1233/98) Subject: Installation of an incinerator

Is the Commission aware of the fact that the Flanders Region, which has responsibility for environmental matters in Belgium, is planning to install a waste incineration unit on the edge of the Brussels Region?

Under European law, is it possible for such a unit to be installed so close to the most densely populated part of the country?

Has the Commission received (and, if so, has it replied to) any communication from the Brussels regional government?

 Answer

The Commission has received several complaints in relation to this incinerator and is aware of the decision taken in this matter by the competent Belgian authorities.

Having been informed of these complaints, the Commission intends to examine the case in January 1999 and will decide whether it would be appropriate to initiate proceedings against Belgium for violation of Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

The Brussels Region has the opportunity, as does any other natural or legal person, to lodge a complaint with the Commission, if it considers that it has to draw the Commission"s attention to a possible violation of Community law. In such case, it would have the right, like any other plaintiff, to confidentiality.

Question No 71 by Jens‐Peter Bonde (H−1235/98) Subject: Early retirement pension in Denmark

Is the new Danish early retirement pension scheme consistent with the Commission's understanding of Community law?

Will it be possible for wage earners who have paid contributions to the new scheme to transfer those contributions to an early retirement insurance scheme in another country? Will it also be possible to receive payment pro rata of the state's contribution to the early retirement pension if one has made a total of 25 years' contributions to an unemployment fund and/or an early retirement pension scheme in another Member State?

If one has paid 25 years' contributions in Denmark, is it possible to have the early retirement pension paid in another Member State and, if so, have it taxed in a new country of residence?

 Answer

The honourable Member has asked a question which relates to the compatibility of Denmark"s revised early retirement pension scheme (’efterløn") with Community law.

According to the information at the disposal of the Commission, any person has the right to receive a Danish early retirement pension from the age of 60, provided that person has contributed to the Danish unemployment scheme for at least 20 out of the last 25 years (or 10 out of the last 15 years if that person was born before 1 March 1992 and has been affiliated to a Danish unemployment organisation for the period from 31 March 1992 up to the age of entitlement to the pension) and provided that person resides in Denmark.

The Commission has received a number of complaints from citizens of Member States who have worked in Denmark and have been refused payment of this pension on the grounds that either they were no longer resident in Denmark, or they did not fulfil the conditions relating to the minimum insurance period in Denmark.

Although this benefit does not fall within the scope of Regulation 1408/71, the Commission is currently looking into whether it is consistent with Community law that this benefit cannot be exported and that the principle of aggregation is not applied in granting this benefit.

The Commission has sent a letter to the Danish Government in which it asks for further information and for its comments on this matter.

The Commission is currently examining the reply received from the Danish authorities and will keep the honourable Member informed of the action it intends to take in response to this.

Question No 72 by Inger Schörling (H−1237/98) Subject: ECHELON

One of the subjects discussed during a debate in Parliament on 14 September was the ECHELON surveillance system. The Commission said on that occasion that it had no knowledge of the existence of ECHELON. It would, therefore, be valuable to know what emerged during the Council meeting on justice and home affairs of 3‐4 December 1998, particularly with regard to covert listening and monitoring of telecommunications and how that relates to ENFOPOL 98, 10951/98 and 10951/1/98 REV 1 amending the Council resolution of 17 January 1995(12) .

Why does the Commission not find it appropriate to discuss these matters openly with Parliament, particularly in view of the fact that they concern the individual's right to a private life and that any agreements concluded may be in direct conflict with Member States' national legislation?

 Answer

As far as the Commission is concerned, the honourable Member is referred to the answers to written questions E−1039/98 from Mrs Van Dijk, E−1040/98 from Mrs Van Dijk, E−1306/98 from Ms Muscardini and others, E−1429/98 from Ms Raschhofer, E−1776/98 from Mr Manisco, E−1987/98 from Mr Kaklamanis and oral question H−1067/98 from Ms McKenna.

Question No 73 by Maj Theorin (H−1240/98) Subject: Afghanistan

First and foremost I should like to express my appreciation of the Commissioner's commitment to the cause of women in Afghanistan. The situation is dreadful and untenable and the Union must demonstrate in practical terms that we do not accept what is happening in the country.

Earlier this year I asked the Commission what measures it intended to take in support of the women of Afghanistan. The Commission replied that Community aid would be seriously reviewed if there was no improvement very soon. No such improvement has occurred and I would, therefore, ask in what way Community aid has now been altered and whether the Commission considers that these measures are sufficient?

 Answer

Since the honourable Member's question H−0660/98 in July 1998, to which she refers, and as she indicates in her present question, there has been no identifiable improvement in the overall situation in Afghanistan or in the policies of the Taliban towards the treatment of women.

This has indeed resulted in the rethinking of Community aid policies towards Afghanistan as set out in the Commission's reply to the Member's earlier question. This action has been carried out in the context of a similar policy review by the international donor community within the Afghan Support Group, which includes Member States, other bilateral donors, EC and concerned UN agencies with a view to arriving at coherent concerted policies on aid delivery in the current circumstances.

In view of the deterioration of working conditions in Kabul, the Commission has decided to suspend all its programmes to the city until the authorities allow NGOs to work in a principled manner and to deliver aid without any discrimination. In the meantime, aid continues in rural areas, but is administered in the country by local Afghan partners, on a temporary basis. As far as the Commission is concerned, a few winter relief and medical programmes are being implemented in Hazarajat and the North, with expatriates.

The Commission is continuing to monitor the situation in Afghanistan closely through the EC Delegation in Islamabad and the EC Coordination Office in Peshawar, Pakistan.

Question No 74 by Sören Wibe (H−1241/98) Subject: Trade war between EU and USA

A number of small and medium‐sized firms in Sweden will be affected by the trade war between the Union and the USA. It is particularly harsh given that Sweden wanted no part in this conflict as our trade policy on bananas caused no problems at all with the WTO before Sweden became a Member State of the Union. A settlement must be reached soon. The trade war is causing unemployment to rise in Sweden.

Are there any plans to compensate those firms – in Sweden, for example – that will be affected by the USA's 100 % penalty tariffs? Is there are possibility of Sweden being able to opt out of the Union's quota scheme for bananas and thereby escape the USA's trade reprisals?

 Answer

The Commission is making every effort possible to avoid any US sanctions from entering into force. The Commission considers that the Community has faithfully implemented the recommendations of the WTO Dispute Settlement Body in the bananas case by taking the necessary measures to bring the EC banana regime into conformity with WTO rules. The WTO conformity of these implementing measures is, as for any trade measures, open to examination by a WTO panel. In absence of any condemnation by a WTO panel of those measures, it is our view that any US request to the Dispute Settlement Body for authorisation to suspend concessions against the EC is inadmissible. The measures proposed by the US are therefore unilateral actions outside the WTO rules.

There are no plans, at least not at the level of the European Commission, to compensate European firms that would be affected by a possible US suspension of concessions in respect of EC exports. Whether any such plans exist at the level of the Member States is not known to the Commission.

There is no legal possibility for Sweden to opt out of the Community regime for the imports of bananas.

Question No 75 by Ioannis Theonas (H−1242/98) Subject: Plans to build and operate a nuclear power station in Akkuyu in Turkey

Plans to build and operate a nuclear power station in Akkuyu in Turkey are causing particular concern among the Eastern Mediterranean peoples. The chief danger arises from the major risk of earth tremors but also in connection with the management of effluent and the use of the plutonium produced. These fears are leading to increased doubts regarding the advisability of investment in this project, given that, according to official information released by the Turkish authorities, the cost, compared with the expected benefit, will be particularly high, since the projected power station is expected to supply only 2 to 3 % of the country's future energy needs. In addition, the project runs directly counter to the current of opinion at international level, questioning the advisability of nuclear power stations and leading to the shutting down of many of them, including eight in Canada, which use the same technology as the power station being proposed in Turkey, together with others in Germany.

What steps will the Commission take to end the projected construction and operation of the nuclear power station in Akkuyu, which would endanger the surrounding area and infringe international commitments concerning the protection of the environment and public health from dangers arising from the use of nuclear energy?

 Answer

The plan to build the nuclear power station in Akkuyu aims to respond to the growing demand for civilian energy that has been forecast in Turkey for the years to come. The start of energy production is forecast for the year 2006.

In its first regular report on Turkey"s progress towards accession, which was adopted on 4 November 1998, the Commission pointed out that the construction of nuclear power stations in Turkey should enable bottlenecks to be removed and should promote diversification of energy sources. It also pointed out in the same report that the decision to build the nuclear power station in Akkuyu would require an evaluation of the possible repercussions in relation to the existence of an earthquake zone relatively close to the chosen site.

The information gathered by the Commission points to the fact that the Akkuyu site was chosen by the Turkish authorities after in‐depth seismic, geological and environmental studies were carried out by specialised departments at several Turkish universities.

In any event, the contractual framework of relations between the Community and Turkey does not permit the Commission to cooperate with Turkey in matters of nuclear energy.

Moreover, the European Commission does not have the competence to participate in the selection of nuclear sites. It is the Turkish authorities and, in particular, the ‘Turkish Atomic Energy Authority" (TAEA) who are responsible for making the selection, within the guidelines of the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA).

Furthermore, this project concerns power stations built outside the territory of the Community, therefore the Community directives obviously do not apply.

In its communication on a European strategy in support of Turkey, adopted on 4 March 1998, the Commission put forward a proposal for cooperation in environmental matters, in order to set in motion sustainable development in this country and to include this sector in the other economic policies. Such cooperation would entail intervention on the part of the Community budget. On 21 October the Commission adopted two proposals for regulations to allow financial support for the European strategy. The Commission hopes that the budgetary authority can approve these proposals in as short a time as possible.

Question No 76 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−1244/98) Subject: Protection of the Sounion historic park

The Sounion historic park, covering an area of 750 hectares is being threatened by property development turning it into a summer resort, and resulting in unauthorised building activities. In addition to providing a vital green belt within Attiki, an area which suffers from severe environmental pollution, this national park is an historic site containing archaeological remains and in particular the famous temple of Poseidon on Sounion promontory.

Has the Commission provided funding for programmes seeking to restore and preserve Sounion park and will it take more necessary measures to protect it? What steps will it take to prevent property development in this area?

 Answer

The Sounion National Park (SNP) covers the biggest part of a Site of Community Importance proposed by the Hellenic authorities, according to Directive 92/43/EEC(13) , to be included in the European ecological network Natura 2000. This site hosts a number of priority habitats and species and, therefore, any activity that could seriously affect its integrity should be avoided in view of its future inclusion into the Natura 2000 network.

The Commission has not provided so far any specific funding for nature conservation purposes in the SNP. The protection of that site from any potentially harmful activities, including property development, falls within the responsibility of Hellenic competent authorities which should ensure that its conservation value is maintained. The Commission could intervene in case of a clear breach of Community legislation.

Question No 77 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−1245/98) Subject: Greek ‘health and welfare' optional operational programme

In view of existing shortcomings concerning hospital equipment and inadequate infrastructures for the development of training programmes for medical personnel under the existing health‐care system, can the Commission say what have been the take‐up rates to date for each of the five measures under the health sub‐programme?

 Answer

The take‐up rate of Community appropriations under the ‘health and welfare" operational programme (OP) currently stands at 62 % in terms of commitments and 40 % in terms of payments as a proportion of the total budget of the Structural Funds (EUR 225 million) which was agreed for the period 1994‐1999.

Public spending within the Community, as declared to the Commission, for the entire OP up to 25 September 1998, was EUR 63.824 million.

As far as the five measures of the single ‘health" sub‐programme is concerned, the following amounts were declared:

‐ Measure 1, basic infrastructure and equipment: EUR 43.390 million

‐ Measure 2, infrastructure for research: EUR 1.714 million

‐ Measure 3, emergency medicine: EUR 8.427 million

‐ Measure 4, information systems: EUR 1.088 million

‐ Measure 5, blood collection: EUR 0.228 million

Question No 78 by John Cushnahan (H−1247/98) Subject: Riding helmet standards

Has the Working Group 5 on standards met to discuss the safety issues relating to equestrian helmets, with particular reference to document N326, as requested by Parliament's Petitions Committee?

 Answer

Given that helmets for equestrian activities are items of personal protective equipment covered by Directive 89/686/EEC, the Commission instructed the CEN to draw up a harmonised standard indicating compliance with the basic health and safety requirements laid down in that Directive.

The resulting standard – EN 1384, 'Helmets for equestrian activities' – was adopted in September 1996 and its reference was published in the Official Journal of the European Communities of 14 June 1997.

The Commission would point out that, while all helmets must meet the requirements laid down in the Directive, implementation of which is compulsory, manufacturers are not obliged to comply with standards, which are always of a voluntary nature.

Mr Downes submitted a petition seeking to improve the safety of riding helmets, with particular reference to the protection afforded by standard EN 1384, which he considers inadequate. The Commission fully supports this initiative.

Although standards are drawn up by European standardisation bodies, which are private organisations to which the Commission may not give orders, it expressly asked CEN/TC 158, which is responsible for this standard, to review at the earliest opportunity the requirements laid down for riding helmets.

The CEN/TC 158 technical committee and its working group agreed that there was a need to improve the standard for helmets for equestrian activities and took immediate action towards this end.

Specialised working group CEN/TC 158 WG 5 met on 14 October 1998 to consider the substance of the standard on the basis of document CEN/TC 158 N 326, to which the honourable Member's question refers. The petitioner and Mr Costello, who drew up that document, attended the meeting. The next meeting is to be held on 3 March 1999.

The Commission is monitoring the standardisation work extremely closely with a view to ensuring that a solution acceptable to all is found in the form of a helmet which affords riders greater protection.

Question No 79 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−1249/98) Subject: Failure to bring Greek law into line with Community directives on the liberalisation of telecommunications

The Greek Government has failed to incorporate into its legislation the following directives concerning the liberalisation of telecommunications: 95/62/EC(14) , 97/13/EC(15) , 97/33/EC(16) and 95/51/EC(17) and has incorporated only in part the following directives: 97/51/EC(18) , 90/388/EC(19) , 96/2/EC(20) and 96/19/EC(21) . As a result of this delay, the Commission has suspended Community funding for the ‘telecommunications' operational programme. Can the Commission say what are the consequences for Greek consumers of the failure of Greece to comply with the above directives and what additional measures will the relevant Community authorities take if Greece fails to comply on time with Community directives?

 Answer

The objective of the liberalisation and harmonisation directives is to open markets to competition and to create the conditions for the establishment of a single European telecommunications market. As stated in the Commission's Fourth Report on the implementation of the telecommunications regulatory package, adopted on 25 November 1998(22) , this is intended on the one hand to contribute to growth and employment, and on the other to provide users and consumers with a wider choice of service providers, improved quality of service, an opportunity to take advantage of the development of new technologies, and the benefit of lower tariffs.

The Commission considers that, given the delays in Greece in implementing in particular the directives on interconnection, licensing, voice telephony and leased lines, and to a certain extent those relating to the liberalisation of telecommunications markets, consumers in Greece are deprived of the full benefit of the advantages referred to above.

The Commission has consistently stressed, not least in the Fourth Report, that it will act where Member States fail to take the necessary measures to implement the telecommunications directives.

The Court of Justice has to date delivered one judgment against Greece for failure to implement; there are currently five proceedings ongoing in respect of the harmonisation directives and three in respect of the liberalisation directives.
